        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1335107520" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer490075118577605503">Music, Tarot cards, Victorian Paris, and the supernatural. Nice combination. Add family saga and you’ve got a winner for my money. Mosse alternates a highly detailed (overly so) saga of Leonie Vernier and her family (1890s) with the modern day Meredith Martin (2007) who is researching the biography of Claude Debussy. I found this back and forth between these two time periods to weaken the story and suspense because the Meredith storyline was so boring. And the constant backstories and long infor</span>
  <span id="freeText490075118577605503" style="display:none">Music, Tarot cards, Victorian Paris, and the supernatural. Nice combination. Add family saga and you’ve got a winner for my money. Mosse alternates a highly detailed (overly so) saga of Leonie Vernier and her family (1890s) with the modern day Meredith Martin (2007) who is researching the biography of Claude Debussy. I found this back and forth between these two time periods to weaken the story and suspense because the Meredith storyline was so boring. And the constant backstories and long information dumps didn’t add any sparkle to the story. But clearly Mosse’s talents remain strong in the historical which were beautifully written, sometimes overwritten at 572 pages. Atmospheric? Yes. Provocative? Yes. Mosse is a master at descriptive prose. The ending was anticlimactic so I was disappointed after such a long and tedious read. Good story telling for the most part, but I’m betting all could have been said in 350 pages. I will add that Mosse’s The Winter Ghost (298 pages) is a better read and a tightly spun supernatural adventure.</span>
  <a data-text-id="490075118577605503" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1284822867" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1284822867" />
    <a title="Thea " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9301662-thea"><img alt="Thea " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574872222p2/9301662.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1284822867?book_show_action=true">May 19, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thea " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thea " href="/user/show/9301662-thea">Thea </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780752893440" href="/book/show/1992920.Sepulchre">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1284822867" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7926427288018897460">"Love—true love—is a precious thing. It is painful, uncomfortable, makes fools of us all, but it is what breathes meaning and color and purpose into our lives."<br/><br/>The research done for this book is amazing. And because of that, the ambience is fantastic. It captured 1890s Paris with its equally beautiful writing stunningly. <br/>The character department didn't disappoint either. I felt for every one of them, and I must say that this has the most evil (though mortal) villain I've encountered so far.<br/>The s</span>
  <span id="freeText7926427288018897460" style="display:none">"Love—true love—is a precious thing. It is painful, uncomfortable, makes fools of us all, but it is what breathes meaning and color and purpose into our lives."<br /><br />The research done for this book is amazing. And because of that, the ambience is fantastic. It captured 1890s Paris with its equally beautiful writing stunningly. <br />The character department didn't disappoint either. I felt for every one of them, and I must say that this has the most evil (though mortal) villain I've encountered so far.<br />The story jumps from past to present, yet it's never a bother because both stories are compelling. It all comes to a full circle, and it's lovely ❤<br />Clearly, I enjoyed this book a lot. Although it's unlike anything I've ever read, I wouldn't say it's everyone's cup of tea. It requires patience, and even I found some parts to be dragging. But if you love Paris, classical music, tarot cards and drama, you'll enjoy this a lot :)</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2012967891?book_show_action=true">Jun 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Molly Ringle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Molly Ringle" href="/user/show/2440492-molly-ringle">Molly Ringle</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2012967891" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14090618386041252780">An atmospheric ghost story with a pinch of Da Vinci Code. Though I didn't get emotionally invested, I kept reading for the enjoyment of the French setting and the interesting tarot and musical angles. I'd have edited some things differently if it were down to me--we know we're in France speaking French, and that this is all in translation so to speak, so why sprinkle in so many phrases in French? It's like when there's a film set in France in which the characters are all meant to be speaking Fre</span>
  <span id="freeText14090618386041252780" style="display:none">An atmospheric ghost story with a pinch of Da Vinci Code. Though I didn't get emotionally invested, I kept reading for the enjoyment of the French setting and the interesting tarot and musical angles. I'd have edited some things differently if it were down to me--we know we're in France speaking French, and that this is all in translation so to speak, so why sprinkle in so many phrases in French? It's like when there's a film set in France in which the characters are all meant to be speaking French but it's done in English, but they put on French accents with their English. Argh; doesn't make sense. But I digress. The book kept me interested and even got me to look up a cool little tarot-card app for my phone, just to admire the artwork and ponder the symbolism for each card that comes up.<br /><br />P.S. I read this without having read the previous book, Labyrinth, and didn't feel I was missing any info. So feel free to embark on it even if you haven't read that one!</span>
  <a data-text-id="14090618386041252780" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer254085356" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5569856303669052759">A surprisingly quick read, despite the thickness of the book! Sepulchre is quite entertaining and I actually enjoyed it a lot more than Mosse's first book, Labyrinth. That said, the plot is a bit clumsy sometimes and the present-day heroine is not a very interesting character. One more thing: there are <b>so many</b> mistakes in the French text that I'm wondering if anyone actually copy-edited the book (if they did, their French is obviously very poor!). There are also quite a few clichés about life in</span>
  <span id="freeText5569856303669052759" style="display:none">A surprisingly quick read, despite the thickness of the book! Sepulchre is quite entertaining and I actually enjoyed it a lot more than Mosse's first book, Labyrinth. That said, the plot is a bit clumsy sometimes and the present-day heroine is not a very interesting character. One more thing: there are <b>so many</b> mistakes in the French text that I'm wondering if anyone actually copy-edited the book (if they did, their French is obviously very poor!). There are also quite a few clichés about life in France, which was annoying at times (I haven't seen anyone smoke a Gauloise in the past 20 years but I guess it adds to the French "ambiance" of the book!). Still, this was an entertaining read.</span>

        <a title="Samidha; समिधा" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Samidha; समिधा" href="/user/show/43362597-samidha">Samidha; समिधा</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43362597-samidha?shelf=historical-fantasy">historical-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43362597-samidha?shelf=part-of-a-series">part-of-a-series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43362597-samidha?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43362597-samidha?shelf=cover-is-love">cover-is-love</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43362597-samidha?shelf=women-s-writing">women-s-writing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43362597-samidha?shelf=i-can-try">i-can-try</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43362597-samidha?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/43362597-samidha?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2470756927" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10038066040140354124">Okay, now that I’ve taken some time to think about this book, I might as well review it. <br/><br/>So, WHAT THE HELL? I am more than a little annoyed with historical fantasy as a genre, it always disappoints me. The former half of the narrative never meets with the latter. Do authors forget their own stories? Are they trying to pull a Dickens? Do they get money by the word? Why do I have to sit through 500 pages worth of sludge only to find out that I would’ve understood the denouement even if I had only </span>
  <span id="freeText10038066040140354124" style="display:none">Okay, now that I’ve taken some time to think about this book, I might as well review it. <br /><br />So, WHAT THE HELL? I am more than a little annoyed with historical fantasy as a genre, it always disappoints me. The former half of the narrative never meets with the latter. Do authors forget their own stories? Are they trying to pull a Dickens? Do they get money by the word? Why do I have to sit through 500 pages worth of sludge only to find out that I would’ve understood the denouement even if I had only read the jacket? <br /><br />The three stars are basically for the setting. The novel was very atmospheric and I could feel myself being in both 1891 Paris and then 2007 Europe. That is all. <br /><br />The initial visions, hauntings, images, paranormal activities, even the concept of music - all of these successive intrigue scenes - like the Tarot Shop, or the Bousquet family, all of that was sold off as a clue to a larger family history. <br /><br />Ugh, NO. Much like The Historian, I felt the end to be so anticlimactic. This could’ve easily been a three hundred page novel. All the characters and the ultimate “incident” that is supposed to be the unraveling of the “true heiress” was introduced/had taken place within the first two hundred pages. A hundred pages more and it could’ve been wrapped up easily. <br /><br />Usually when I give three stars, the book is above average. It’s better, can be worked on. This book however made me feel deflated. I literally slouched in my chair when I came to the big reveal. I felt so cheated, the entire narrative was building up to this huge,dark secret which was supposed to have all sorts of elements of the fantastical and the medieval, and instead it gave me absolute shit in place of that. <br /><br />I didn’t even want to review it because I felt that I’d been wronged. Call me dramatic, but this book reminded me that some ideas are just shit, and never should make it to print.<br /><br />Ugh, I want my hours back!</span>
  <a data-text-id="10038066040140354124" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16714095393434710914">After labyrinth, this book was a kind of a major let down. All that information was also not new to me, as I have already researched quite a bit about Tarots. The information itself was pretty rudimentary, nothing deep or fascinating.<br/>The major problem with this work was that, Mosse clearly had a nice interesting vision or premise in her mind. She inept at transferring that vision to paper or else, her vision was as skewed as her tale. There was too much of repetetion, lots of cliches, very predi</span>
  <span id="freeText16714095393434710914" style="display:none">After labyrinth, this book was a kind of a major let down. All that information was also not new to me, as I have already researched quite a bit about Tarots. The information itself was pretty rudimentary, nothing deep or fascinating.<br />The major problem with this work was that, Mosse clearly had a nice interesting vision or premise in her mind. She inept at transferring that vision to paper or else, her vision was as skewed as her tale. There was too much of repetetion, lots of cliches, very predictable story line, boring and slow narrative, too familiar characterization. There is nothing that lingers on... I took absolutely nothing from Sepulchre.<br />Mosse, sadly, was unable to awaken any haunting quality through her words, which is a sine qua non of a book of this kind.<br />The saving grace was the language. No errors or lacunae there.</span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4246571-laura?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4246571-laura?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4246571-laura?shelf=hunting-for-lost-object">hunting-for-lost-object</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer418186023" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2209918683474673143">“Sepulchre” tells double story – one set in 1791, other in 2007<br/><br/>From the author of the bestseller “Labyrinth” (which I have not read), comes a chubby novel that mixes many elements of lost treasures, a crazed jilted lover bent on revenge, supernatural dabblings, romance, and a search for roots. <br/><br/>Meredith Martin is the modern-day researcher who is working on a biography of the French composer Debussy. Martin travels to Paris to acquaint herself with the places Debussy lived. She is especially inter</span>
  <span id="freeText2209918683474673143" style="display:none">“Sepulchre” tells double story – one set in 1791, other in 2007<br /><br />From the author of the bestseller “Labyrinth” (which I have not read), comes a chubby novel that mixes many elements of lost treasures, a crazed jilted lover bent on revenge, supernatural dabblings, romance, and a search for roots. <br /><br />Meredith Martin is the modern-day researcher who is working on a biography of the French composer Debussy. Martin travels to Paris to acquaint herself with the places Debussy lived. She is especially interested in tracking down Lilly, who lived with him when he received acclaim for his work, Peleas and Melisande. <br /><br />Her second reason for coming to France is to track down her French roots. She has a photograph and a scrap of music, and knows that her family lived in the south of France in the Carcassone region. Wouldn't you know it, but her roots lead her directly to a old mansion, now converted into the hotel she stays at, the Domaine de la Cade. “Cade” is the French word for the junipers that grow on the grounds. <br /><br />The mansion is also the scene of the story that takes place in 1791, when Anatole Vernier and his sister Leonie flee Paris to seek safety in an aunt's home (the Domaine) which has fallen into disrepair as well as disrepute. <br /><br />The Vernier siblings, Anatole and Leonie, and their aunt Isolde, are pursued by a murderous aristocrat bent on revenge on Isolde for jilting him. They make their home in Isolde's manse in Carcassone, but their happiness is interrupted by the aristocrat's duel with Anatole (a fatal one for Anatole, and nearly so for Isolde, too, when the scoundrel pulls out a second gun and shoots at her). The women are heartbroken by his death, but manage to carry on. <br /><br />It is difficult to summarize the charm of a novel that moves at a slow pace, that must be savored like a nice French wine. This is not a potboiler, and not a thriller that takes you on a breakneck ride through a simple plot. This is a candidate for future classic status, a novel as involving as anything written by dead authors from the nineteenth century. <br /><br /><br />SEPULCHRE, KATE MOSSE, G P PUTNAM'S SONS, NEW YORK, 2007, (first published by Orion Publishing Group), 565 pages. ISBN: 978-0-399-15467-6</span>
  <a data-text-id="2209918683474673143" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_137907506" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/137907506" />
    <a title="Magill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2884323-magill"><img alt="Magill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1278887023p2/2884323.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/137907506?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Magill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Magill" href="/user/show/2884323-magill">Magill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2884323-magill?shelf=harmless-enough">harmless-enough</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer137907506" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9113237032747504738">I gave this book a 2, although maybe a 2.5 as, in comparison to Labyrinth and Winter Ghosts, I was not nearly as aggravated by the style and characters. By the way, although the books are touted as a trilogy they are really unrelated except for location and, what some others have called, time-slip. So the order read, or even not reading at all, is no actual loss to the reader. In fact, this story itself was weakened by the forced inclusion of the entire time-slip attempt, as there was plenty of </span>
  <span id="freeText9113237032747504738" style="display:none">I gave this book a 2, although maybe a 2.5 as, in comparison to Labyrinth and Winter Ghosts, I was not nearly as aggravated by the style and characters. By the way, although the books are touted as a trilogy they are really unrelated except for location and, what some others have called, time-slip. So the order read, or even not reading at all, is no actual loss to the reader. In fact, this story itself was weakened by the forced inclusion of the entire time-slip attempt, as there was plenty of story in the characters themselves even using the tarot cards, sheet music and photos and the link connecting past and present.<br /><br />Given the previous paragraph, for the story as written, I think the ... less fleshed-out characters were a positive in this book as I found things dragged on in Labyrinth and the main characters got annoying. In this book Meredith and Hal aren't very well developed but their roles are simpler and less to get bogged down with (although there were some weird things, like Meredith writing her notes in the third book of a 6-pack she got at borders or some store, I mean, I really don't think that counts as characterization). The Leonie story-line? Well, since she was 17 I gave her some slack as to her actions but they still seemed to serve the plot more than anything else, not particularly consistent in my mind. <br /><br />Although descriptions were over-written and explained as usual, I didn't find them quite as distracting or jarring this time as in previous books. But I admit that I read the book in my own order, I read 2-3 sections of Leonie's story and then would go back and read Meredith's. It didn't seem to make the slightest difference to the development of the story and, as the story was somewhat simpler than Labyrinth, I felt like I was moving through the tome more easily and without as much jumping back and forth. The endings were weak but, given that there really wasn't all that much to work with, not surprising. Perhaps the author should set aside her timeslip stories and focus on her characters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9113237032747504738" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

And so is the killer.\n\n\nDon’t miss the hottest thriller of the summer – perfect for readers who loved The Holiday.\n‘I loved it – absorbing and wonderfully twisty. A unique combination of popular and elite influences, the music of this island nation has fascinated since the golden age of the son -- that New World aural collision of Africa and Europe that made Cuban music the rage in Paris, New York, and Mexico beginning in the 1920s.Originally published in 1946 and never before available in an English translation, Music in Cuba is not only the best and most extensive study of Cuban musical history, it is a work of literature in its own right. Drawing on such primary documents as obscure church circulars, dog-eared musical scores pulled from attics, and the records of the Spanish colonial authorities, Music in Cuba sweeps panoramically from the sixteenth into the twentieth century. Carpentier covers European-style elite Cuban music as well as the popular rural Spanish folk and urban Afro-Cuban music.\n\nIn a substantial introduction based on extensive original research, Timothy Brennan explores Carpentier\'s career prior to the writing of his novels. Looking especially at Carpentier\'s work as a music reviewer, radio producer, and musical theorist, Brennan suggests new ways of thinking about the role of Latin American artists in Europe between the <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8771008127474623905">Kate Mosse is an international bestselling author with sales of more than five million copies in 42 languages. Her fiction includes the novels Labyrinth (2005), Sepulchre (2007), The Winter Ghosts (2009), and Citadel (2012), as well as an acclaimed collection of short stories, The Mistletoe Bride & Other Haunting Tales (2013). Kate’s new novel, The Taxidermist’s Daughter is out now.<br/>Kate is the Co-</span>
  <span id="freeText8771008127474623905" style="display:none">Kate Mosse is an international bestselling author with sales of more than five million copies in 42 languages. Her fiction includes the novels Labyrinth (2005), Sepulchre (2007), The Winter Ghosts (2009), and Citadel (2012), as well as an acclaimed collection of short stories, The Mistletoe Bride &amp; Other Haunting Tales (2013). Kate’s new novel, The Taxidermist’s Daughter is out now.<br />Kate is the Co-Founder and Chair of the Board of the Baileys Women’s Prize for Fiction (previously the Orange Prize) and in June 2013, was awarded an OBE in the Queen’s Birthday Honours List for services to literature. She lives in Sussex.</span>

          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Love - true love - is a precious thing. It is painful, uncomfortable, makes fools of us all, but it is what breathes meaning and color and purpose into our lives.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
